  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_da95e6e2-df28-435a-96c6-d96e8ab92fa3'
      });

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer16239832692159216217">A literary work by the Hungarian author Imre Madách, first published in 1861. A play composed in verse, it is today a staple of Hungarian theater and has been translated and adapted into many languages and media. The play follows Adam and Eve as they appear in various guises in episodes throughout history and grow in self-awareness and wisdom.</span>

<div id="review_77012857" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/77012857" />
    <a title="Jim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1895570-jim"><img alt="Jim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1484874117p2/1895570.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/77012857?book_show_action=true">Nov 07, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jim" href="/user/show/1895570-jim">Jim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789631531398" href="/book/show/2019707.Az_ember_trag_di_ja">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer77012857" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12741283120169536646">Although considered one of the hallmarks of Hungarian literature, Imre Madách's <b>The Tragedy of Man</b> is virtually unknown in the West (or the East for that matter). Imagine a work resembling Milton's <b>Paradise Lost</b> and Goethe's and Marlowe's recounting of the Faust legend. Adam and Eve have been cast out of Paradise. Instead of slinking away as he does in Genesis, Lucifer takes Adam through different periods of history, and even into a Fourierist future in which everyone lives in Phalansteries run </span>
  <span id="freeText12741283120169536646" style="display:none">Although considered one of the hallmarks of Hungarian literature, Imre Madách's <b>The Tragedy of Man</b> is virtually unknown in the West (or the East for that matter). Imagine a work resembling Milton's <b>Paradise Lost</b> and Goethe's and Marlowe's recounting of the Faust legend. Adam and Eve have been cast out of Paradise. Instead of slinking away as he does in Genesis, Lucifer takes Adam through different periods of history, and even into a Fourierist future in which everyone lives in Phalansteries run by scientists -- and even beyond into the wastes of outer space. <br /><br />Adam always has hope that somewhere there is a future that gives life meaning. Lucifer's role is to constantly dash Adam's hopes into the dust. In the end, Lucifer wafts Adam back to his hut where he lives with Eve outside of Eden; and God puts in a minatory appearance, warning Adam that some knowledge is not appropriate for him. But isn't that why Adam and Eve ate of the forbidden fruit?<br /><br />Madách's work is given me a lot to think about. It is on one hand a dark work, and on the other a splendid one that celebrates the quest of man for meaning in his life. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12741283120169536646" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1007657529" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1007657529" />
    <a title="Chiek Er" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6148306-chiek-er"><img alt="Chiek Er" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1314621917p2/6148306.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1007657529?book_show_action=true">Jul 26, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chiek Er" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chiek Er" href="/user/show/6148306-chiek-er">Chiek Er</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6148306-chiek-er?shelf=european-classics">european-classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1007657529" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17805598799776215528">Perhaps the greatest poem ever written. Steep in theology, history and philosophy, this beautiful poem is my first book by an Hungarian author. Thanks to my colleague Attila for introducing this piece of priceless literature. The breath and scope of the tale stretches from Alpha to Omega, planet earth to outer space. With God, Lucifer, Adam and Eve as the central characters in the book, To get a taste of the future, Lucifer, Adam and Eve would travel through time to different epochs of history, </span>
  <span id="freeText17805598799776215528" style="display:none">Perhaps the greatest poem ever written. Steep in theology, history and philosophy, this beautiful poem is my first book by an Hungarian author. Thanks to my colleague Attila for introducing this piece of priceless literature. The breath and scope of the tale stretches from Alpha to Omega, planet earth to outer space. With God, Lucifer, Adam and Eve as the central characters in the book, To get a taste of the future, Lucifer, Adam and Eve would travel through time to different epochs of history, cities, political situations and ideals pursued by mankind. They play key historical roles (mostly by Adam) and witnessed first hand the future which was more bleak &amp; gruesome than pleasant. Yet the future cannot change because of God's will on the fate of mankind. God is after all at the top of the food chain. The man may eat the Seal, but God will have absolute authority over each and every man's journey and individual's ultimate end. <br /><br />Reminiscent of the book Job, Adam, Lucifer and Eve returned from their time travel to the garden of Eden, embittered by what they saw. Yet It was most encouraging for Adam that God will support the man in his struggles because He cares for His creation. It is after all not the end but the journey itself that is the goal as Adam had rationalised to himself before his return to Eden. To strive righteously despite the sufferings one meets along the way. That is the goal. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17805598799776215528" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer2340807880" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer1387663785885359832">Love, love, love this one ^.^ It's every thing that Milton's Paradise Lost is not & provides a unique twist on the Faust legend. Hmm... could this be my favourite Faust adaptation?? The philosophy, theology, journey through time & reincarnation elements could make me favour this over the others. <br/><br/>The poetic lines are steeped in meaning that resonates more with each glance. Basically, this little drama is by a Hungarian author. Madach took Adam & Eve on a journey through all of human history with </span>
  <span id="freeText1387663785885359832" style="display:none">Love, love, love this one ^.^ It's every thing that Milton's Paradise Lost is not &amp; provides a unique twist on the Faust legend. Hmm... could this be my favourite Faust adaptation?? The philosophy, theology, journey through time &amp; reincarnation elements could make me favour this over the others. <br /><br />The poetic lines are steeped in meaning that resonates more with each glance. Basically, this little drama is by a Hungarian author. Madach took Adam &amp; Eve on a journey through all of human history with Satan following along every step of the way. That alone gives the reader much to ponder on. Each era raises slightly different ethical/philosophical questions about the human condition &amp; society. At it's heart though, it's a love story between Adam &amp; Eve. What more could you ask for in such a tiny book? And the ending, just when you thought it couldn't get any better. * bliss * It's a great book for religious, Biblical folks &amp; for the secular, philosophical musing folks. <br /><br />I must buy a copy of this for my own library &amp; look more into these various debates it brings up. There is a free edition on the archives site. I read it by a different translator though: J.C.W. Horne. Horne's language is simply exquisite with that old Biblical language that compliments the story so well. Adam &amp; Eve are the main characters after all!<br /><br />It's widely celebrated as the best Hungarian book. Please volunteer more loved Hungarian books in the comments, I love to be challenged in this way. Or if you know of a better Faustian adaptation.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1387663785885359832" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Jc Er" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33616567-jc-er"><img alt="Jc Er" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1407060182p2/33616567.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1276368598?book_show_action=true">May 10, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jc Er" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jc Er" href="/user/show/33616567-jc-er">Jc Er</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33616567-jc-er?shelf=european-classics">european-classics</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1276368598" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6632171294707936413">Magnificent poetry from Hungarian's most famous 19th century poet, Imre Madach. This is heavy literature is steep in theology, history and philosophy. God, Adam, Satan and Eve are the main characters. After the fall of Adam and Eve when they ate the apple, Satan took Adam on a time travel to visit men's future (our history now) to see what will come to be. Instead of being a bystander, Adam took on important historical figures like a pharaoh in Egypt, a Roman General, a knight crusader, Kepler t</span>
  <span id="freeText6632171294707936413" style="display:none">Magnificent poetry from Hungarian's most famous 19th century poet, Imre Madach. This is heavy literature is steep in theology, history and philosophy. God, Adam, Satan and Eve are the main characters. After the fall of Adam and Eve when they ate the apple, Satan took Adam on a time travel to visit men's future (our history now) to see what will come to be. Instead of being a bystander, Adam took on important historical figures like a pharaoh in Egypt, a Roman General, a knight crusader, Kepler the astronomer in Czechoslovakia and an astronaut in space gazing down on our blue earth that was much less polluted when Madach wrote this poem 200 years ago compared to now. 6000 years later, Adam is an Eskimo trying to survive a global ice age. Adam would be whisked back to the Garden of Eden to face God to acknowledged that the fate of mankind for 6000 years was flashed before his eyes. The fate of mankind to be unfolded according to the Script written by God. Just like our fates. We are actors to the script of God. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6632171294707936413" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Lukács" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18677172-luk-cs"><img alt="Lukács" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1455790326p2/18677172.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1945386030?book_show_action=true">Mar 18, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lukács" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lukács" href="/user/show/18677172-luk-cs">Lukács</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789631531398" href="/book/show/2019707.Az_ember_trag_di_ja">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1945386030" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17432445430791612632">I am not sure what language I should write this review, but since most people understand english, english it is.<br/><br/>I've read this book in high school for Hungarian literature class. In short it fallows Adam's path through big events of history to present and future, accompanied by Lucifer. Lucifer tries to prove Adam that life has no purpose, everyone will eventually die and no-one can make a difference. Lucifer shows Adam how great ideas are born, implemented in practice and become the opposite of</span>
  <span id="freeText17432445430791612632" style="display:none">I am not sure what language I should write this review, but since most people understand english, english it is.<br /><br />I've read this book in high school for Hungarian literature class. In short it fallows Adam's path through big events of history to present and future, accompanied by Lucifer. Lucifer tries to prove Adam that life has no purpose, everyone will eventually die and no-one can make a difference. Lucifer shows Adam how great ideas are born, implemented in practice and become the opposite of themselves because of human nature. I could write pages about this book, and not just because we analysed it inside-out in school, but also because I loved it.<br /><br />Definitely worth a read if you are into classics. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17432445430791612632" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1742205509" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1742205509" />
    <a title="Liz Quack" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/53701146-liz-quack"><img alt="Liz Quack" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1742205509?book_show_action=true">Aug 29, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Liz Quack" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Liz Quack" href="/user/show/53701146-liz-quack">Liz Quack</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1742205509" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13156553103310104285">Spoiler Alert<br/><br/><br/><br/><br/><br/><br/><br/><br/><br/><br/><br/><br/><br/>First I'll say what I enjoyed about this book. I quite liked Lucifer, he was funny and clever. He was the one voice of reason in this story. That being said, I had trouble keeping with it when Lucifer's parts were minimal, Adam was a drag, and Eve was silenced just as much as any woman coming out of a book from this time. I enjoyed the imagery and was excited when I thought that Adam had died while floating in Space, unfortunately he was revived and able to drive the final nail into</span>
  <span id="freeText13156553103310104285" style="display:none">Spoiler Alert<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br />First I'll say what I enjoyed about this book. I quite liked Lucifer, he was funny and clever. He was the one voice of reason in this story. That being said, I had trouble keeping with it when Lucifer's parts were minimal, Adam was a drag, and Eve was silenced just as much as any woman coming out of a book from this time. I enjoyed the imagery and was excited when I thought that Adam had died while floating in Space, unfortunately he was revived and able to drive the final nail into the coffin for this book.<br /><br />Sadly the last section of The Tragedy of Man managed to disgust me to the point that I was almost unable to finish reading it. The last stage of Adam’s journey took place in the decimated wasteland that earth had become. Much to Adam’s distress, it was discovered that men did survive but as “caricatures” of men… The last surviving humans were Eskimos and apparently that was the true tragedy. The fact that an indigenous person, able to inhabit the world free from some of the oppressions and limitations that us colonizers have placed on them, was not presented to have an optimistic future, depressed me greatly. Compared to the previous act, which was a wonderful float through space, this was a mean spirited joke. I don’t recommend this book based solely on the end. We’re better than this.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13156553103310104285" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_88412338" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/88412338" />
    <a title="Seth" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1027428-seth"><img alt="Seth" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1242221122p2/1027428.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/88412338?book_show_action=true">Feb 05, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer88412338" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8498545943289874617">A Hungarian Paradise Lost. Quite thought-provoking!<br/><br/>From a critique at the end of the work by Mihaly Szegedy-Maszak:<br/>"The message of Madach [seems to be:] that unqualified faith in any system of ideas is self-destructive. . . . Unless we are content with existing on the level of animals or automata, we must strive to be independent of the ruling opinions of the time and should not fear to enter into the most hostile relationship with the existing order, because fate will never take us from the res</span>
  <span id="freeText8498545943289874617" style="display:none">A Hungarian Paradise Lost. Quite thought-provoking!<br /><br />From a critique at the end of the work by Mihaly Szegedy-Maszak:<br />"The message of Madach [seems to be:] that unqualified faith in any system of ideas is self-destructive. . . . Unless we are content with existing on the level of animals or automata, we must strive to be independent of the ruling opinions of the time and should not fear to enter into the most hostile relationship with the existing order, because fate will never take us from the responsibility and even the obligation of thinking for ourselves."<br /><br />The story makes one wrestle with such things. Well worth the read, especially alongside Paradise Lost.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8498545943289874617" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer6938110610252332945">In this monumental play Madách takes his protagonist Adam from the Fall through all the major epochs and settings of history, including Egypt, Athens, Constantinople, Prague, and London all the way to futuristic utopias, to experience the major philosophical, social, political and existential struggles of humanity. While each surrounding situation presents different types of challenges, the constant struggle is always based on the ever present conflict of the human experience. A true masterpiece</span>
  <span id="freeText6938110610252332945" style="display:none">In this monumental play Madách takes his protagonist Adam from the Fall through all the major epochs and settings of history, including Egypt, Athens, Constantinople, Prague, and London all the way to futuristic utopias, to experience the major philosophical, social, political and existential struggles of humanity. While each surrounding situation presents different types of challenges, the constant struggle is always based on the ever present conflict of the human experience. A true masterpiece.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer899993185" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9525032777123312274">One of the few set texts I read for school. A powerful and monumental play, somewhat similar to Milton's Paradise Lost. Adam and Eve, cast out from Paradise, are led by Lucifer through various moments of humanity's history, to learn whether human existence has a meaning and purpose. While their hopes are crushed in each scene, they never cease dreaming about and fighting for a better future.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('/s4qUZVCrkC9Cif4URI576b+RpkEn6kDQSIwxOS88Hdg1zs7NXXRr8qUNXRF5GKeqmH7UBylwndyPjnJRNmO7w==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('dXSmYYwloPFKC5V3xb93j1yBJMhmQTD2jBypMQNeVsTrbbcLLBLfHj2Vh/vRSSz+UB6ZAX57W4K/AKA8ozsoXA==')})
  }
//]]>

Chance brings him into a fabulous fortune, and from then on, he succeeds in all ventures he undertakes, and everything he touches turns to gold: his only failure, and one which comes to turn his riches to dross, is his marriage. In his search for happiness, he comes to hate his wealth. He finally escapes from his double life, and finds refuge on a wild island in the Danube where the love of a young girl, Noémi, gives real meaning to his existence.\n\nThe Man with the Golden Touch is a jewel of both Hungarian and world literature, and is indeed one of the most beautiful romantic novels.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/350638.Imre_Mad_ch">About Imre Madách</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/350638.Imre_Mad_ch"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1259223768p3/350638.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/350638.Imre_Mad_ch">Imre Madách
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
17
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer5249583371941569085">Imre Madách de Sztregova et de Kelecsény was a Hungarian writer, poet, lawyer and politician. His major work is The Tragedy of Man (Az ember tragédiája, 1861). It is a dramatic poem approximately 4000 lines long, which elaborates on ideas comparable to Goethe's Faust. The author was encouraged and advised by <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/198571.J_nos_Arany' rel='nofollow noopener'>János Arany</a>, one of the most famous of 19th century Hungarian poets.<br/><br/>He was born in Alsószt</span>
  <span id="freeText5249583371941569085" style="display:none">Imre Madách de Sztregova et de Kelecsény was a Hungarian writer, poet, lawyer and politician. His major work is The Tragedy of Man (Az ember tragédiája, 1861). It is a dramatic poem approximately 4000 lines long, which elaborates on ideas comparable to Goethe's Faust. The author was encouraged and advised by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/198571.J_nos_Arany" rel="nofollow noopener">János Arany</a>, one of the most famous of 19th century Hungarian poets.<br /><br />He was born in Alsósztregova, the Kingdom of Hungary (today Dolná Strehová, Slovakia) in 1823. The Madách family was able to trace their descent as far back as the 12th century; with a medieval knight, a Turk-beating hero and a Kuruc officer recorded down the line of the family tree. But a poet was also remembered; Gáspár Madách from the 17th century. And the ties of kindred could be traced to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/2959063.Mikl_s_Zr_nyi" rel="nofollow noopener">Miklós Zrínyi</a>, the poet and soldier. <br />From 1829 Madách studied at the Piarist school of Vác. During a cholera epidemic he stayed in Buda in 1831. In 1837 he began his studies at the university of Pest. In 1842 he officially becomes a lawyer. In 1860 he finished working on the "The Tragedy of Man". He died in Alsósztregova, the Kingdom of Hungary in 1864.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5249583371941569085" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/638050-az-ember-trag-di-ja">Quotes from The Tragedy of Man</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Let me see no more of my harsh fate: this useless struggle.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;

            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;You reason like a well-fed man, while your comrade's philosophy comes from an empty stomach. With reason you won't vanquish one another. You would agree at once if either you were hungry or he were full. The animal is always first in you and only when it is appeased, Man becomes conscious and in his great pride, despises his real nature.&rdquo;</span>
